<div id="review_2224955081" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2224955081" />
    <a title="Neil" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11192244-neil"><img alt="Neil" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1435376678p2/11192244.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2224955081?book_show_action=true">Dec 25, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Neil" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Neil" href="/user/show/11192244-neil">Neil</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780070089273" href="/book/show/2325777.Fail_Safe">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11192244-neil?shelf=alternate-history">alternate-history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11192244-neil?shelf=classic-fiction">classic-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11192244-neil?shelf=end-of-the-world">end-of-the-world</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11192244-neil?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11192244-neil?shelf=finished-in-2017">finished-in-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11192244-neil?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11192244-neil?shelf=military-fiction">military-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11192244-neil?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11192244-neil?shelf=technothriller">technothriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2224955081" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15177502530419743859">This was an interesting book; it strongly reminded me of both <i>Red Alert!</i> (another novel) and the movie, <i>Dr. Strangelove, or How I Learned To Stop Worrying And Learned To Love The Bomb</i>. It moves at a decent pace; the character development is decent. It is set in the historical mid-1960s and has the names of individuals in it who were alive during that time. It is also a bit of a polemic, of sorts, about the dangers of completely trusting every decision to machines and "removing" human responsibil</span>
  <span id="freeText15177502530419743859" style="display:none">This was an interesting book; it strongly reminded me of both <i>Red Alert!</i> (another novel) and the movie, <i>Dr. Strangelove, or How I Learned To Stop Worrying And Learned To Love The Bomb</i>. It moves at a decent pace; the character development is decent. It is set in the historical mid-1960s and has the names of individuals in it who were alive during that time. It is also a bit of a polemic, of sorts, about the dangers of completely trusting every decision to machines and "removing" human responsibility from "the equation." <br /><br />It is a pretty crazy book, in that it takes place primarily at SAC headquarters, in Omaha, NE, and in the War Room in Washington, D.C. There are occasional snippets from the Commander of Flight Group Six as well, but they occur infrequently. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ This may or may not be considered a "spoiler," but on the chance that it is: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The novel is a "What if . . . ?" scenario in that it attempts to answer the question, "What would happen if a flight of American bombers is accidentally sent into Russia to bomb Moscow?" It also attempts to answer how a global thermonuclear war might be avoided if the bombers are successful with their mission <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> There is really not a lot of "action" in the book; most of the book centers around people talking<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[: professors talking in college classrooms; academics talking in social settings about their theories on global thermonuclear war; military personnel talking about the situation at hand; the American President speaking with Kruschev over the phone <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />There were a couple of parts of the book that did get me a little confused. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[It involves the speed of the Vindicator bombers and the speed of the Russian fighters and missiles. On page 174, it says that the American Skyscrapper fighters have a top speed of 1600 mph, 50 mph above the Vindicator bombers (which obviously means the Vindicators have a top speed of 1550 mph). On page 204, it is said that one of the Vindicators and a Russian fighter approaching head-on have a combined speed of 3000 mph; this means the Russian fighter is doing 1450 mph. The Vindicator drops a Bloodhound missile to launch it at the Russian fighter. An American Air Force general estimates the speed of the Bloodhound missile to be 1500 mph. The missile has to be going faster than that for it to be able to shoot down the Russian fighter; otherwise, the Vindicator would be ahead of the missile it just dropped and in danger of being struck by its own missile. I do not recall anywhere where it says the Vindicators slowed down after entering Russian air space, either, so the Vindicators are still flying at full speed towards Moscow. <br /><br />Then, on page 205, a Vindicator is destroyed by a Russian missile. The Russian air-to-air missile is described as being a "slow missile which compensates by a long range." How can this missile be so slow it can compensate its lack of speed by having a longer range than estimated and yet still be able to catch a Vindicator bomber to destroy it? Perhaps I am making assumptions on the speed of the Russian missiles based on comments in the book that are less clear than I think, and that is what is making me confused about this Russian "slow missile." <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />On a sided note, I added it to my "technothriller" shelf as it does have some discussions about advanced technology in it. That being said, it does not have the infodumps that future "technothrillers" will have in them. Still, though, it does take the technology currently available at the time and extrapolates forward as to what America could possibly have developed in terms of its military might.<br /><br />I enjoyed reading the book. I have read some other books that had similar endings to this one, so the ending was not quite as much of a shock as it might have been, otherwise. At the same time, the ending is quite horrific, on various levels <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[i.e. - two men casually discussing the destruction of an American city and five million Americans to stave off a war that will kill everybody on the planet; the President's wife will be in NYC for some sort of charity when the bombs go off; the families of various characters are also in NYC and will die immediately after Moscow is destroyed <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. This book does a "good job" of discussing the variables that McNamara and his "Whiz Kids" seemed unable to completely grasp with their using equations to attempt to define the reality they wanted as opposed to the reality that existed at the time.<br /><br />It was a good book; I am glad that I finally got around to reading it.<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15177502530419743859" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3458914452" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3458914452" />
    <a title="Jean Bonilla" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50197341-jean-bonilla"><img alt="Jean Bonilla" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3458914452?book_show_action=true">Jul 24, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jean Bonilla" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jean Bonilla" href="/user/show/50197341-jean-bonilla">Jean Bonilla</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3458914452" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9079449494226872669">When I last read ‘Fail-Safe’ in the mid-1960s, it felt horribly relevant to the world as it was. Two pivotal things have changed since then. One is that the Soviet Union no longer exists. Another is that the spread of nuclear weapons into so many hands creates a totally different kind of threat that demands a more complex foreign policy. <br/><br/>Even before its fall, we now know that the USSR was a hollow shell of a nation. That fact didn’t prevent them from amassing a huge stockpile of nuclear weapons,</span>
  <span id="freeText9079449494226872669" style="display:none">When I last read ‘Fail-Safe’ in the mid-1960s, it felt horribly relevant to the world as it was. Two pivotal things have changed since then. One is that the Soviet Union no longer exists. Another is that the spread of nuclear weapons into so many hands creates a totally different kind of threat that demands a more complex foreign policy. <br /><br />Even before its fall, we now know that the USSR was a hollow shell of a nation. That fact didn’t prevent them from amassing a huge stockpile of nuclear weapons, however, so ‘Fail-Safe’ is not wrong in structuring this plot. Today’s very different environment would mean a threat that comes from small, targeted strikes, not a big, all-out ‘Doomsday’ finish. So, it demands a very different political response. The sad thing is that a book like this is only possible when we do face a 1950s-style threat of us-against-them. The foreign policy I engage in every day looks at threats that include WMD, disease, economic attacks (including trade wars), and climate change. We have to wield the weapons in our arsenal somewhat differently. The forces against us are just as capable of destroying New York through a 20 megaton bomb, Ricin, Ebola, bank failures, famine, or a tsunami. We must stand ready to respond not just to the first, but to all of these - even all at the same time! <br /><br />I also didn’t realize back then that the book came out before Kennedy was shot; published in 1962, it postulated a world where he would still be President in 1968! The authors don’t mention him by name, but use him as their model. They characterize the President in a way that treats him as a normal politician and a typical leader and they exhibit none of the semi-reverential tone that his assassination provoked in post-1963 works. It’s odd for me to see him in this story with 2020 eyes, because I’m sure he’s intended to come across as an heroic figure and, instead, he’s “regular.” One other odd feature of the book I noticed: people didn’t seem overly concerned about radiation unless it was very close by. The pilots attacking Moscow mentioned it, but I think the nuclear weapons were on the Soviet side. I would think that New York would be a hot-spot for years if they used nuclear bombs there, but perhaps 80 megatons can be standard explosives. <br /><br />The book dedicated a lot of time to philosophical debates about man vs. machine. It wanders off into lengthy descriptions of the backgrounds of various characters that are neither fundamental to the action nor intrinsically interesting. At certain points, the writing borders on stilted. The Dream alone plays an effective role, weaving that one character’s horror into what ultimately must be done. <br /><br />Last, but certainly not least, when I read the book before, I hadn’t seen ‘Dr. Strangelove!’ The whole time I was reading, the song ‘We’ll Meet Again’ ran through my head. It made a poignant soundtrack for a somewhat tragic tale of lives lost to a blown transistor. <br /><br />All that said, it definitely turned out to be not a one-, a two-, or a three-, but a six-Kleenex book. Be ready with a box if you read it!</span>
  <a data-text-id="9079449494226872669" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1348381435" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1348381435" />
    <a title="Randal Schmidt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24700139-randal-schmidt"><img alt="Randal Schmidt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528982918p2/24700139.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1348381435?book_show_action=true">Jan 20, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Randal Schmidt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Randal Schmidt" href="/user/show/24700139-randal-schmidt">Randal Schmidt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/41718846-fail-safe">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1348381435" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15946382665662398347">Definitely worth the read for anyone interested in American foreign policy and 20th century culture. <br/><br/>This book is a rehash of the themes in <i>Red Alert</i> by Peter George (the novel basis for Dr. Strangelove), and in fact, so similar was <i>Fail-Safe</i> perceived to be that it brought a lawsuit for copyright infringement (settled out of court eventually, if memory serves). However, after reading the two close together, while I think I can understand the lawsuit's basis (surely it was the trading of city fo</span>
  <span id="freeText15946382665662398347" style="display:none">Definitely worth the read for anyone interested in American foreign policy and 20th century culture. <br /><br />This book is a rehash of the themes in <i>Red Alert</i> by Peter George (the novel basis for Dr. Strangelove), and in fact, so similar was <i>Fail-Safe</i> perceived to be that it brought a lawsuit for copyright infringement (settled out of court eventually, if memory serves). However, after reading the two close together, while I think I can understand the lawsuit's basis (surely it was the trading of city for city in the two books, the most blatant repetition), the similarities are not as numerous as you might expect, and the inciting incidents are very different. In some ways, <i>Fail-Safe</i> is a superior book: it spends more time on character development, the scenes with the president (unnamed, but heavy New England accent, son of wealth, etc.) and Khrushchev are more historically believable, and the character of Groteschele is a fantastic (in a love-to-hate-him kind of way) addition.<br /><br />If General Quinten from <i>Red Alert</i> was the standout character of that novel, then Groteschele, the smug professor and lecturer on nuclear strategic theory, is the standout here. His self-satisfied, elitist attitude and his faith in his own inhuman theories provides a frightening glimpse into what a powerful amoral person can become. He reminded me of Ellsworth Toohey from <i>The Fountainhead</i> by Ayn Rand in that he was desirous of power and influence and would do whatever it took to talk his way into power over other men. All the while, he is a worm and a coward and his contributions are worse than nothing, his theories worse than wrong—they are dangerous. For the addition of Groteschele alone, this book is worth reading as he highlights the absurdity and the moral bankruptcy of nuclear theory.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15946382665662398347" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1903458270" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1903458270" />
    <a title="Ulysses" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/392640-ulysses"><img alt="Ulysses" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1547929080p2/392640.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1903458270?book_show_action=true">May 13, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ulysses" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ulysses" href="/user/show/392640-ulysses">Ulysses</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780440124597" href="/book/show/1972170.Fail_Safe">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/392640-ulysses?shelf=to-reread">to-reread</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1903458270" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4444460473649694095">What if nuclear holocaust were brought about not by an intentional act of war or a malevolent artificial intelligence (a la Skynet), but by a simple component failure in a computer system in which its human operators-- and their foes-- placed too much trust? This is the scenario of Fail-Safe, which was written in 1962, amidst the hysteria of the Cold War. The natural inclination for both Americans and Soviets at the time may have been to focus their fears on "the other side" as the primary threa</span>
  <span id="freeText4444460473649694095" style="display:none">What if nuclear holocaust were brought about not by an intentional act of war or a malevolent artificial intelligence (a la Skynet), but by a simple component failure in a computer system in which its human operators-- and their foes-- placed too much trust? This is the scenario of Fail-Safe, which was written in 1962, amidst the hysteria of the Cold War. The natural inclination for both Americans and Soviets at the time may have been to focus their fears on "the other side" as the primary threat to their continued existence, but the forward-looking had started to consider whether perhaps the opposing powers' reliance on nuclear deterrence and computerized defense systems might be an equal or perhaps greater danger.<br /><br />It is fascinating that despite the era in which it was written, this story has no villains. The interactions between the Americans and the Soviets are portrayed in a manner that is strikingly humane and sympathetic to all characters. Both sides are portrayed as victims not only of the impending disaster, but of the mistrustful, Mutually Assured Destruction-based world order that made such a disaster possible, and which everyone, even up to the US President and the Soviet Premier, is powerless to stop. <br /><br />The book contains more "personality exposition" for a few key characters in the early chapters than is necessary to meet the needs of the story. However, once these details are taken care of, the plot quickly accelerates to full speed and never lets up the rest of the way. For anyone who enjoys political or military thrillers, this book is a classic and a must-read. Despite the aforementioned unnecessary exposition in the early chapters, it's a quite short book, and basically impossible to put down once the bombers cross the go line.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4444460473649694095" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('tEwaVwQPNnnavGjWivA6HnRECYs/TjfMM+YbyPLTa4YVoJD9xoCqHnbahBZlhUmPDqb3Tt8xS9l+ktLANP1lBA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('6kt7v04pCgjjxanLuZ3xued3EAdImsLqL7f+LY7iD29Lp/EVjKaWb0+jRQtW6IIonZXuwqjlvv9iwzclSMwB7Q==')})
  }

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_50489327'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/50489327-if-then?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">If Then: How the Simulmatics Corporation Invented the Future<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/77397.Jill_Lepore?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Jill Lepore<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.90 avg rating &mdash; 364 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2020\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer10034572680745714425\">The Simulmatics Corporation, launched during the Cold War, mined data, targeted voters, manipulated consumers, destabilized politics, and disordered knowledge—decades before Facebook, Google, and Cambridge Analytica. Jill Lepore, best-selling author <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText10034572680745714425\" style=\"display:none\">The Simulmatics Corporation, launched during the Cold War, mined data, targeted voters, manipulated consumers, destabilized politics, and disordered knowledge—decades before Facebook, Google, and Cambridge Analytica. Jill Lepore, best-selling author of These Truths, came across the company’s papers in MIT’s archives and set out to tell this forgotten history, the long-lost backstory to the methods, and the arrogance, of Silicon Valley.\n\n\nFounded in 1959 by some of the nation’s leading social scientists—“the best and the brightest, fatally brilliant, Icaruses with wings of feathers and wax, flying to the sun”—Simulmatics proposed to predict and manipulate the future by way of the computer simulation of human behavior. In summers, with their wives and children in tow, the company’s scientists met on the beach in Long Island under a geodesic, honeycombed dome, where they built a “People Machine” that aimed to model everything from buying a dishwasher to counterinsurgency to casting a vote. Deploying their “People Machine” from New York, Washington, Cambridge, and even Saigon, Simulmatics’ clients included the John F. Kennedy presidential campaign, the New York Times, the Department of Defense, and dozens of major manufacturers: Simulmatics had a hand in everything from political races to the Vietnam War to the Johnson administration’s ill-fated attempt to predict race riots. The company’s collapse was almost as rapid as its ascent, a collapse that involved failed marriages, a suspicious death, and bankruptcy. Exposed for false claims, and even accused of war crimes, it closed its doors in 1970 and all but vanished. Until Lepore came across the records of its remains.\n\n\nThe scientists of Simulmatics believed they had invented “the A-bomb of the social sciences.” They did not predict that it would take decades to detonate, like a long-buried grenade. But, in the early years of the twenty-first century, that bomb did detonate, creating a world in which corporations collect data and model behavior and target messages about the most ordinary of decisions, leaving people all over the world, long before the global pandemic, crushed by feelings of helplessness. When Castner returned home to his wife and family, he began a struggle with a no less insidious foe, an unshakable feeling of fear and confusion and survivor’s guilt that he terms The Crazy. His thrilling, heartbreaking, stunningly honest book immerses the reader in two harrowing and simultaneous realities: the terror and excitement and camaraderie of combat, and the lonely battle against the enemy within—the haunting memories that will not fade, the survival instincts that will not switch off. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer7133406716661029628">Eugene Burdick was an American Political Scientist and co-author of <i>The Ugly American</i> (1958), <i>Fail-Safe</i> (1962) and <i>The 480</i> (1965).<br/><br/>He was born in Sheldon, Iowa. His family moved to Los Angeles, California, when he was age 4. Burdick attended Stanford University and Oxford University where he earned a Ph.D. degree in psychology, and he worked at the department of Political Science at the University </span>
  <span id="freeText7133406716661029628" style="display:none">Eugene Burdick was an American Political Scientist and co-author of <i>The Ugly American</i> (1958), <i>Fail-Safe</i> (1962) and <i>The 480</i> (1965).<br /><br />He was born in Sheldon, Iowa. His family moved to Los Angeles, California, when he was age 4. Burdick attended Stanford University and Oxford University where he earned a Ph.D. degree in psychology, and he worked at the department of Political Science at the University of California. In 1956, his critically acclaimed novel <i>The Ninth Wave</i>, a Houghton Mifflin Literary Fellowship winner, was published. At the end of the 1950s, he was among the first members of the Society for General Systems Research. He died suddenly of a heart attack at the age of 46.</span>
